To you, three dear sons, I have completed this book which is the last of the science of medicine along with its completion, and I have finished it through its explanations and by its declarations. And it came to me in a vision that I should complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the work of hands, that is to say "surgery".
And because the work of hands is neglected in our region and is foreign to everyone, thus the science is lost and decayed, and nothing remains of it except the small descriptions in books of the ancients that the hands have moved. And from this has come the error and the vanity, and the intentions are closed, and the science is obscured.
It seems to me that I want to revive it along with the organization of this treatise according to the way of exposition and declaration and of abbreviation; and I will come together with the forms of the iron cauterizations, and other instruments of the work with the adjustment of the declaration and preparation of them, as it is necessary.
And for the reason that no good master of hands is found in our time, the art of medicine is extended. And it is necessary for the one who undertakes to learn in the science of anatomy, as Galen said, so that he knows the judgment of the members, and the forms and the conjunctions and separations of them, and has knowledge of the bones and nerves and brains and the number of them, and the movement and rest of the veins, both moving and resting, and their issue.
And for this Hippocrates said that the name is not much, and with the work is little; and we place in the art of hand.
We tell you in the introduction of this book that whoever is not knowledgeable about the things previously mentioned, should beware so that they do not fall into error and that they do not harm men.
that I have seen many times several of those who undertake this science and if they abandon their science and their experiments.
For I saw a foolish surgeon who cut into the aposteme of scrofula on the neck of a woman, and cut the arteries at the neck; because so much blood came out that the woman fell dead in his hands.
And I saw another surgeon who extracted a stone from a man's bladder, and the stone was large; he removed with it a piece of the bladder, and the man died within three days.
And I have already seen it called for by the healer, and I saw the condition of the patient and the size of the stone, and if one touched it he would die.
And I saw another doctor who took in care the duke of our land who had his leg broken, and with the breakage a wound;
and the doctors, complaining of ignorance, held the breakage on the wound by strong binding, and did not leave the wound to form a scab, and they left it thus for a certain time;
and he commanded that it not be untied;
and remained thus until the leg became entirely swollen and the foot, and came to destruction.
I was called to come to him, and I hurried to come and untie the leg; and when I had untied it, he was at peace and did not feel any pain.
But all along the corruption was already confirmed in the member, and it cannot be healed.
and the corruption does not cease to spread through the member as long as the member is entirely lost.
And we see another case where the abscess bursts the swollen tumor, and then becomes inflamed because of the evil of its symptom.
For tumor, when it is full of melancholic humor, does not require anything but to be touched with iron if it is not in a place where one can then remove all that is corrupted.
And for this, dear son, it is necessary that you know that the work of hands is divided into two parts: that is to say, attention, where there is danger in several conditions;
and I will show in each place of this book where there is fear and danger.
For which reason it is necessary that you guard yourself, so that foolish people cannot speak badly of you.
Take your arms in caution, and undertake your illnesses lightly and firmly, and use the better way so that by the grace of God you come to health and are praised;
and let the terrifying diseases of grievous sickness;
and put away from you that which makes you doubt, and have faith in you so that you can come from this world to the other of glory.
Galen says in one of his commentaries: Do not take a foolish doctor to cure your illness, lest you be called a foolish patient.
I have divided this treatise into three chapters.
The first chapter is about cauterizations of fire and about cauterizations of sharp medicine, and this chapter is ordered from the head to the feet;
and of the forms of cauterizations, and of the uses of cautery, and of all that which is necessary in the work.
The second chapter is about cutting and piercing, and of ulcers and of wounds, and of the extractions of arrows, and of similar things;
and it is the chapters put in order and the forms of the treatments.
The third chapter is about restoring dislocations and twists, and similar things;
and it is ordered from the bottom of the feet to the head, and of the forms of the treatments.
Additionally, we remember the work, it is necessary that I describe the quality of the aid and the harm of each complexion.
I say that the qualities of the aid, and of the harm, are a longer speech and subtle science and secret responses;
and of this speaking many of the wise men agree;
and my writings suffice little for their words, for I abhor length.
I say that cauterization helps universally all complexions where there is matter, except for two complexions: that is to know the hot and moist complexion, and the dry and moist complexion.
In a hot and dry complexion with mixed matter, they disagreed about nothing and said that cauterization is of great aid; and the others said the opposite: that cauterization is not suitable for an illness that comes from heat and dryness. For the nature of fire is hot, and it should not be used to cure a hot illness with heat, nor with dryness;
and it is said by those who say the opposite that cauterization provides great aid to hot and dry illnesses that occur in the body of men. For when you examine the body of man and its moisture according to the temperament of fire, you find the body cold.
And I agree with this statement. For I have found it true many times by experimentation.
And let no one interfere with it except he who has experienced it, and who knows through long practice the chapter of cauterizations, and the diversity of complexions of men, and the dispositions and causes of their illnesses, and the occurrences and the space of the tension;
of other complexions we have no fear, namely in cold and moist illnesses. And all the parts gather and agree with the cauterization.
And know, son, that fire cauterization assembles in secret of cures, and has greater goodness than cauterization of burning medicine.
For the fires are simple, and do not come except to the member that is cooked, and do not harm another member unless it is named.
The hot medicines pass from one member to another by touching, and bring to the members diseases of grievous sanitation; and by chance kill the patient.
The fire, due to its height and goodness of its substance, does not do harm, if there is no excess.
And this I have found by experiment and through the length of service and study that I have put into the art, and established on the truth of things;
and for this I excuse myself for the length of the speech, for it does not please me that I withhold from you the secret which is hidden in fire, and the quality of the work, how it expels the diseases of the body, as the philosopher says in demonstrating; Receive it in your thoughts.
And know, son, that they agree on what time the cauterizations should be done, and choose taking pains.
And I say that cauterization is suitable at all times, because the harmful effects that follow are often cured by the aid of cauterization; and likewise the cauterizations are for necessary pains and hastenings that cannot wait for the time when a greater evil may come to the limb.
And do not think ever, dear son, as foolish people do; for the disease that is healed by cauterization has no return; and they say it to him thus;
and they say it to him thus; but it is not so as they believe, because cauterizations are not a medicine that alters the temperament, and dries the humors which are the cause of the pain, and is worth more than medicine for the hastening of its value, and for the virtue of its work and of its authority.
And it is possible that the disease comes from the patient's complexion, at times according to the humor of the disease and its position.
and are gathered in him the excesses by the acquisition of foods and similar things, if it is not for cauterizations administered to him or he has pus of excess and moisture, such as cauterizations of teeth for the pain, and similar things, and such pain does not return. but that happens rarely.
And many people do not understand why cauterization is called the last resort of medicine, and it is true;
but they do not believe that cauterization is worth anything after medicine, but their intentions are false, and cauterization is the last resort of medicine because when we administer methods of cures for a disease and they do not work, then we administer cauterization, and it works.
It appears well that cauterizations are the last resort of medicine, not according to the intention of foolish physicians.
The ancients said that cauterizations with instruments of gold are better than those of iron, and they did not say this because of the quality of the gold or because of the height of its substance;
and they said that the place of cauterization does not become gangrenous, but this is not true because I have experienced it, and found that it is true in some men;
and cauterizations of gold are worth more than those of iron, as they praised them. And however when you heat an instrument of gold, you do not know how hot it is by the color of the gold, and it cools down quickly;
and if one heats it too much; it becomes disfigured by the fire; and then there is enough of an impediment

of the pain of the teeth
When toothache comes from cold, or there is cold or fever, and medical treatments are of no use, then it is necessary to perform cauterization in two ways: and cauterization with a burning instrument and cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with a burning instrument is such that you take the wick of a candle, and the tip in an iron or bronze skewer; afterwards take cotton and envelop it around the tip of a stick, and the marrow in melted wick, and put on the aching tooth quickly and hold until it is cooled;
and do it often until its virtue reaches the root of the tooth, and if you see marrow and cotton in cold melted wax, and put on the aching tooth, and put on the hot iron as long as the fire reaches the decay of the tooth.
The fire cautery is such that you should hold a iron or steel rod, and let it be thick so that the heat does not reach the patient's mouth;
After heating the cautery, so that the form appears afterwards, and put it on the tooth and hold your hand until the cautery has cooled down;
Do this often, for the pain will be gone the next day or the day after.

I say that wounds are diverse according to what they are made of, and according to the limbs or wounds that are affected, and of what they are made.
The things by which wounds are made are the throwing of stones, and the cutting of swords and knives, or of lances or clubs or arrows, or similar things;
or that are made according to the limbs of the body, such as a wound that strikes on the neck, or on the head, or the foot, or the belly, or the liver, and similar members;
and I will tell you the treatment of each wound, and begin with simple head wounds, for the memory will come in its chapter.
I say that when a simple wound comes to the head, and the bone is not broken, take care if it's an injury of stone or similar things, and the skin is entirely broken and the wound is large, and you suspect slightly an abscess of heat: if the wound is so much worse from the head injury that if it comes with a little blood from the wound, and the blood is on the contrary side of the injured part;
and forbid the patient excess of wine and of coarse meats.
And if he does not want to bleed, his food should be diminished, and he should not drink wine, nor eat any meat;
and put on the wound, if abscesses or ulcers come, cotton moistened in rose oil or in tightening wine;
and if there is no hot abscess, then put on the wound, if it is fresh with all its blood, and it has not been moved by air, powder, which I will discuss a little later;
and if the blood has been moved, then put on some ointments, as long as it is healthy.
And if the wound is large like that of a sword and similar, and the lips cannot be assembled by themselves with tape, then assemble them by stitching, as I will describe for wounds of the belly, concerning stitching.
If the wound is uncovered to the bone, and it is narrow, and does not hold except for a little, then cover it; After the cure of the wound with medicine, and restoring the flesh on the skin, instruct the patient to eat coarse foods, such as toasted and roasted meat, like pork, and similar things.

and if an artery is in the wound or vein, without cutting, and it cannot be staunched with medicine, seek out the artery, and if you find it dislocated, then tie it with a thread. and if this does not help, then cauterize it.
And if these wounds are small and simple, then the treatment is light: and it is that you put in this powder which dries just as the arsenic treats the wound.
And the method of the powder is thus: Take a part of incense; blood of dragon two parts; living salts three parts, and powder subtly, and apply to the wound, and extract warmly, until it is well joined and united with all its blood;
and then bind it well, and leave it unbound, so it does not swell; for one should not remove the medicine until it is healed.
And sometimes one makes this with salts alone, when one does not have incense or blood of dragon, but that the arsenic has not moved the wound.

of rupture of the belly
The ruptures of the belly are great and soft and small;

And it is known that the rupture of the belly is in four manners:

I say that when the wound is small, and any part of the intestine comes out, and it's a grievous thing to put back, it is done by one of the two methods: or because of the smallness of the wound, as we have said, or because the intestine is weakened by the cold.

and this is the common manner of stitches, and it is that you take a needle or several, according to the quantity of the width of the wound, then leave at the head of the wound the thread of two fingers, and attach a needle where it has a strong thread; and pierce the two sides of the leather with the two sides of the suture, which is beneath the inner leather, until you make it pierce from that part, and bring together the two ends of the suture to the four ends assembled;
Then strain through a double thread within the needle of two parts, then assemble the two edges of the wound together, and then let the suture tighten, and fix another needle.

The second treatment is common, and it is that you should assemble the two parts of the skin and the two parts of the fascia together with a needle or that it has one strong middle thread;
and when you have pierced these pieces all four together, then return the needle to the part where you began, in the manner that one sews clothes;
and put a space between each stitch the thickness of a little finger;
This stitching is suitable for a man, and I sewed a wound that a man had in his belly, which was injured by a blow, and the wound was more than a palm in width, and it gushed out intestines as much as two palms, and the wound was in the middle of the belly.

Now I will tell you the two proper methods of suturing:
take the needle, and pass it through the part from the outside to the inside;

of a fistula or whichever it may be Be aware that every wound or abscess, when it suppurates, is called universally "fistula", and especially when it is hollow and has a cavity.
And a fistula is according to the true notion a false and hard and white node, where one does not feel pain, and is as hollow as the bone of a bird;
and sometimes in a clear and hollow place, and other times in a thick one; sometimes little, and other times enough;

Here begins the book of fractures These chapters are often necessary in the art of medicine, and are for the restoration of fractures and dislocations.
Know, dear son, that foolish people reject this chapter, for they have never seen any of the ancient books, nor have they learned from letters;
and for this reason the science is foreign in our land, because I have never found any good there;
and I know nothing, except that by the science of ancient books, which I have studied so much that I have treated the science, and by experiment, and by the habit of my life;
and I have described to you in this chapter what is contained, and I have abbreviated it at the end, and I have clearly shown the forms of the instruments that are needed, as I have done in these two chapters before.
It is necessary then that we say in the prologue of this book the sum of the word and the chapters where the wise man puts his understanding, and remains on the truth, so that those who are experienced know and learn this noble art, and teach it to others.
I say that when fracture or dislocation or torsions or concussion comes to anyone, it is necessary that you hasten to anoint the patient on one side and the other, unless something prevents you from doing so, as weakness of the body, or infants, or old people. or if it is not too hot for him, nor too cold;
and avoid cold and chilled foods, that is to say cold joints, and meat of goat and of pork, and do not drink wine, and avoid fatty and greasy foods, and do not fill oneself too much with foods that fill the veins with blood, until you are no longer in doubt about a hot abscess, or an accumulation of matter in the limb;
if the patient returns to their previous governance, he will have used it.
And when the broken bone begins to mend, it is necessary that the patient be nourished with foods that promote fat gain, or have viscosity, such as cooked broth in water, and pork, and testicles, and feet, and calf liver, and eggs, and fresh fish, and strong wine, and similar things. For a warm nourishment will repair bones and will be very beneficial for the fracture.
And know that the bones of the elderly and decrepit cannot well mend or reunite according to their nature due to the dryness of the members, nor those which are too tender, such as the bones of small children;
but in order to heal, reset the bone that is broken and tie the large tendons which hold the member together, until it becomes strong as it was before; and not the whole point of his work, and for this reason it is necessary to nourish him with thick foods, as we have said.

And when they are reset equally, and the patient's mouth closes well without softening, then put on dressings moistened with a mixture made of oil rosat and wax, then tie them lightly with soft bindings;
and let the patient sleep on their back, and their head be steady between two cushions, so that they do not move either to the right or to the left, nor make an effort to chew, but take light food, until the pains are reduced, and the jaws are numb;
and then chew what it wants lightly, and does not labor to open the mouth of the jaw and the drinker, as long as the mandible is well cleaned and healthy.
And if the two jaws cannot be put back in place at one hour, and are not converted to their uses, then come fevers and accustomed sweats, and by chance comes a purging of the belly, and by chance vomiting of pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is a sign of a prediction; and many times he whom it happens to, dies within eight days.
The force cannot dislodge what is stuck in the continuity of the chest and the back, and is sometimes dislocated at the thigh, and one can feel it well.

then put on a plaster and bind it, and make it tight;
and the joint which is turned towards the hip cannot be dislocated; if it does not, and if it dislocates again, reset it to its place, as we have said;

Of the dislocation of the shoulder
Know that the shoulder is dislocated in three ways:
of what is it that the dislocation occurs at the part of the joint at the elbow and the second is at the part of the wrist and by chance it dislocates at the upper part, and that happens rarely;
and not dislocated behind at the shoulder, and not dislocated inward for the kidneys;
and often dislocates downward toward the joint, especially to those who have little flesh, for to them it is a sudden and hasty movement;
to those who have sufficient flesh, it happens with effort, and enters with effort.
And by chance it happens to some men a percussion or fracture, and inflames the shoulder with a hot abscess, and they think that it is dislocated because of this.
It is necessary then that you examine it, until you are certain of it; and then begins the treatment.
And it is known that dislocation occurs when it is downward towards the armpit, and there is a difference between the sick shoulder and the healthy one, and you will find between them a noticeable diversity; and you will find at the head of the shoulder a hollow, and below the armpit to the touch the head of the shoulder, as if it were an elbow; and the patient cannot bring his hand to his neck, nor move with any movements;
and also, if it is dislocated upwards, it is not hidden, for you will find it manifestly at the touch;
and this kind of dislocation is not difficult to reduce, when it is recent, or if the sinew is sick.
And the method is that the ministers raise the hand of the patient up high, then put your fingers below the armpit, and lift the joint with force upwards to its place; and the minister raises the hand, and stretches it up, then puts on a dressing. if it comes quickly.
And if it does not come as we have said in the mornings after, then it is fitting that the patient be bathed in warm water; and let the anointing be soothing and softening, such as a decoction of dock and fenugreek and melilot in water, and let it be anointed;
then let the patient be laid on their back, and put on a dressing a bandage of equal thickness, not too soft nor too hard, then let the physician place his heel on the bandage, and lift the head of the shoulder gently, and pull the hand of the patient;
and another minister holds the head of the patient, so that it does not move downwards; That swelling returns now, and if you see it reset in this manner:
and have the bone that is larger than the patient's; and be straight towards the ribcage, and push the shoulder down from the patient's armpit, and lift the armpit up high, so that the patient hangs in the air; and another attendant pulls the patient's hand downwards towards the belly. And if the patient is too lightweight, then hang with him something weighted, and the dislocation returns now.
And the resetting is done in another manner:
and it is that you place a stick on the ground, whose thickness is like a pestle, and is neither too large nor too small; then it should be placed under the patient, but so that he has his shoulders draped upwards, and the patient should be standing from the length of the stick;
Then his hand should be extended downwards from the other side of the arm, and the joint will return to its place quickly;
and if it does not want to return for all these things that we have said, then make this treatment: Take a piece of two arms in length and four fingers in width, which has the head bent back to enter inside near the bottom of the shoulder, according to this shape:
Then tie a cloth around the head of the fist, so that the fist does not harm the patient, then put it under the armpit of the patient;
and extend his arm on the fist of the long one, and tie the shoulder to the fist, and the arm to the head of the hand, then put the arm on a stick of a wide chair, and let the hands hang downwards, and let the body hang from the other side: The joint returns to its place now.
And when it is in place, in whatever manner that may be, then you must put under the shoulder a layer of even wool, then put the plaster mixed with powdered millet, with incense and absorbent vinegar, on the entire shoulder and around, then strain under the shoulder strongly, and turn the linen on the plaster, and wrap the arm at the neck, and let the hand remain in place.
And it is necessary that he moves little, so that the limbs are comforted, for it will heal more quickly;
and then move more after 5 or 7 days, and move it; and if it is firm and not loosened, then it is healed.
And if the joint is out of place due to moisture that has come, or for another reason, then it is necessary that you administer cauterizations to three parts, as it is stated before in the chapter on cauterizations.
And if you do all that, and it dislocates after seven days, and the joint does not hold, and cannot lift his arm upwards, then know that the nerve at the head of the shoulder is cut or stretched or softened; and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
The dislocation that is towards the chest, and the breasts, or behind, if made by impulse, and stretching of hands, and one administers another ointment;
and cures it until it is healthy.
And if after the healing, stiffness comes to the limb, and slowness in movement, then let the patient bathe many times, until that stiffness is alleviated, and it returns to its first nature.
of the dislocation of the thigh

so that it returns to its condition.
The dislocation of the hand
The joint of the hand is dislocated, and its displacement is slight, more than the other joints. However, it is necessary to bring it back to its place quickly, in the same way that it was dislocated, so that the limb is not weakened, or that an abscess does not form.
And the treatment is that you put the bad hand on a table, and extend the good hand, and look at the bad hand equally, so that it puts its palm on the surface of the joint, and bends until it returns.
In any case, it is necessary that you check if it is well in place; Then put the bad hand with the back of its palm on a table for extension and for treatment. And if the dislocation is at the back of the hand, then put the bad hand with its back on the table, and look to put the hand on the same surface of the joint.
If it returns now, it's good. If not, then apply a poultice of warm abscesses and let it be without restarting the treatment.

if there is no other remedy except to stretch by cauterizing the affected part, and it happens by chance that there is nothing else.
And when the arm is in place, put on the plaster that we have mentioned, then stretch it and leave it for 5 hours, then unbind it and leave it thus.

of the dislocation of the knee joint

of the dislocation of the ankle
The ankles are sometimes sprained, and sometimes completely dislocated, inwardly and outwardly.
